The Laplace Transformation II c-12
 
5 
Introduction
Introduction
In this volume we give some examples of the elementary part of the theory of the Laplace transfor-
mation as described in Ventus, Complex Functions Theory a-5, The Laplace Transformation II. The
chapters and the sections will follow the same structure as in the above mentioned book on the theory.
The examples have been collected about 30 years ago from some long forgotten book on applications.
It was then pointed out by the author, and repeated here that one should not uncritically apply the
Laplace transformation in all cases. Sometimes the simpler methods known from plain Calculus may
be easier to apply.
Leif Mejlbro
March 31, 2011
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Appendices
4.1
Trigonometric formulÃ¦
We repeat the formulÃ¦ known from e.g. Ventus, Calculus 1-a, Functions in one Variable. The addition
formulÃ¦ for trigonometric functions are
(15) cos(x + y) = cos x Â· cos y âˆ’sin x Â· sin y,
(16) cos(x âˆ’y) = cos x Â· cos y + sin x Â· sin y,
(17) sin(x + y) = sin x Â· cos y + cos x Â· sin y,
(18) sin(x âˆ’y) = sin x Â· cos y âˆ’cos x Â· sin y.
Remark 4.1.1 One remembers these important rules by noting that cos x is even, and sin x is odd.
Therefore, since cos(x Â± y) is even, the reduction must contain cos x Â· cos y (even times even) and
sin x Â· sin y (odd times odd). Then we shall only remember the change of sign in front of sin x Â· sin y.
Analogously, sin(xÂ±y) is odd, so the reduction must contain sin xÂ·cos y (odd times even) and cos xÂ·sin y
(even times odd). Here there is no change of sign. â™¦
The antilogarithmic formulÃ¦. These are derived from the addition formulÃ¦ above.
sin x Â· sin y = 1
2 {cos(x âˆ’y) âˆ’cos(x + y)},
even,
cos x Â· cos y = 1
2 {cos(x âˆ’y) + cos(x + y)},
even,
sin x Â· cos y = 1
2 {sin(x âˆ’y) + sin(x + y)},
odd.
4.2
Integration of trigonometric polynomials
The task is to ï¬nd the integral

sinm x Â· cosn x dx,
for m, n âˆˆN0.
We shall in the following only consider one single term of the the form sinm x Â· cosn x, where m and
n âˆˆN0, of a trigonometric polynomial, because we in general can ï¬nd the result by linearity.
We deï¬ne the degree of sinm x Â· cosn x as the sum m + n.
When we integrate such a single trigonometric product of degree m + n, we ï¬rst must answer the
following question: Is it of even or odd degree? These two possibilities are then again subdivided
into to subcases, so we have four diï¬€erent variants of method, when we integrate a trigonometric
polynomial.

1) The degree m + n is odd.
